	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Franks</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Frankish people and society. For the political development of the Franks, see <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Franks_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Franks (disambiguation)">Franks (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Franc" title="Franc">Franc</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">Franks</caption><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="nickname" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:115%;"><div lang="la">Franci</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankish_arms.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Frankish arms.JPG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frankish_arms.JPG/220px-Frankish_arms.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frankish_arms.JPG/330px-Frankish_arms.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Frankish_arms.JPG/440px-Frankish_arms.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2587" data-file-height="1470" /></a><div>Aristocratic Frankish burial items from the Merovingian period</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Languages</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Old_Frankish" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Frankish">Old Frankish</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Religion</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Frankish_paganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish paganism">Frankish paganism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Related ethnic groups</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French people</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Franks</b> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Franci</i> or <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">gens Francorum</i>) were a group of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, whose name was first mentioned in 3rd century Roman sources, associated with tribes on the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Rhine" title="Lower Rhine">Lower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Rhine" title="Middle Rhine">Middle Rhine</a>, on the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Later the term was associated with <a href="/wiki/Romanization_(cultural)" title="Romanization (cultural)">Romanized</a> Germanic dynasties within the collapsing <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, who eventually commanded the whole region between the rivers <a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">Loire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>. They then imposed power over many other post-Roman kingdoms and Germanic peoples, and still later Frankish rulers were given recognition by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> as successors to the old rulers of the Western Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Frankish name does not appear until the 3rd century, at least some of the original Frankish tribes had long been known to the Romans under their own names, both as allies providing soldiers and as enemies. The new name first appears when the Romans and their allies were losing control of the Rhine region. The Franks were first reported as working together to raid Roman territory, but from the beginning these raids were associated with attacks upon them from outside their frontier area, by the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, for example, and with the desire of frontier tribes to move into Roman territory with which they had had centuries of close contact.
</p><p>Frankish peoples inside Rome's frontier on the Rhine river included the <a href="/wiki/Salian_Franks" title="Salian Franks">Salian Franks</a> who from their first appearance were permitted to live in Roman territory, and the <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_Franks" title="Ripuarian Franks">Ripuarian</a> or Rhineland Franks who, after many attempts, eventually conquered the Roman frontier city of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and took control of the left bank of the Rhine. Later, in a period of factional conflict in the 450s and 460s, <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a>, a Frank, was one of several military leaders commanding Roman forces with various ethnic affiliations in Roman Gaul (roughly modern France). Childeric and his son <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> faced competition from the Roman <a href="/wiki/Aegidius" title="Aegidius">Aegidius</a> as competitor for the "kingship" of the Franks associated with the Roman Loire forces. (According to <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>, Aegidius held the kingship of the Franks for 8 years while Childeric was in exile.) This new type of kingship, perhaps inspired by <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> represents the start of the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingian">Merovingian</a> dynasty, which succeeded in conquering most of Gaul in the 6th century, as well as establishing its leadership over all the Frankish kingdoms on the Rhine frontier. It was on the basis of this Merovingian empire that the resurgent <a href="/wiki/Carolingian" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingian">Carolingians</a> eventually came to be seen as the new Emperors of Western Europe in 800.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the term <i>Frank</i> came to be used as a synonym for <i>Western European</i>, as the Carolingian Franks were rulers of most of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, and established a political order that was the basis of the European <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_regime" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancien regime">ancien regime</a></i> that only ended with the <a href="/wiki/French_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="French revolution">French revolution</a>. Western Europeans shared their allegiance to the Roman Catholic church and worked as allies in the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> beyond Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, where they still referred to themselves and the Principalities they established as Frankish. This has had a lasting impact on names for Western Europeans in many languages.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From the beginning the Frankish kingdoms were politically and legally divided between an eastern Frankish, more Germanic part, and the western part that the Merovingians had founded on Roman soil. The eastern Frankish kingdom came to be seen as the new "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>", and was from early times occasionally called "<a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>". Within "Frankish" Western Europe itself, it was the original Merovingian or "Salian" Western Frankish kingdom, founded in Roman Gaul and speaking <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, which has continued until today to be referred to as "<a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>" – a name derived directly from the Franks.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_the_Franks" title="Name of the Franks">Name of the Franks</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A.D._400-600,_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_(1882).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG/220px-A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG/330px-A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG/440px-A.D._400-600%2C_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_%281882%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1822" data-file-height="2392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A.D._400-600,_Franks_-_025_-_Costumes_of_All_Nations_(1882).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 19th century depiction of different Franks (AD 400–600)</div></div></div>
<p>The name <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Franci</i> was not a tribal name, but within a few centuries it had eclipsed the names of the original peoples who constituted it. Following the precedents of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Grimm" title="Jacob Grimm">Jacob Grimm</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> the name of the Franks has been linked with the English adjective "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/frank" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:frank">frank</a>", originally meaning "free".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> There have also been proposals that Frank comes from the Germanic word for "<a href="/wiki/Javelin" title="Javelin">javelin</a>" (such as in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">franca</i> or <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">frakka</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Words in other Germanic languages meaning "fierce", "bold" or "insolent" (German <i lang="de" title="German language text">frech</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">vrac</i>, Old English <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">frǣc</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norwegian" title="Old Norwegian">Old Norwegian</a> <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">frakkr</i>), may also be significant.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eumenius" title="Eumenius">Eumenius</a> addressed the Franks in the matter of the execution of Frankish prisoners in the circus at <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a> by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> in 306 and certain other measures:<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Ubi nunc est illa ferocia? Ubi semper infida mobilitas?</i> ("Where now is that ferocity of yours? Where is that ever untrustworthy fickleness?"). <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Feroces</i> was used often to describe the Franks.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Contemporary definitions of Frankish ethnicity vary both by period and point of view. A <a href="/wiki/Formulary_(model_documents)" class="mw-redirect" title="Formulary (model documents)">formulary</a> written by <a href="/wiki/Marculf" class="mw-redirect" title="Marculf">Marculf</a> about 700 AD described a continuation of national identities within a mixed population when it stated that "all the peoples who dwell [in the official's province], Franks, Romans, Burgundians and those of other nations, live&#160;... according to their law and their custom."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Writing in 2009, Professor <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wickham" title="Christopher Wickham">Christopher Wickham</a> pointed out that "the word 'Frankish' quickly ceased to have an exclusive ethnic connotation. North of the <a href="/wiki/Loire_(river)" class="mw-redirect" title="Loire (river)">River Loire</a> everyone seems to have been considered a Frank by the mid-7th century at the latest (except <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>); <i>Romani</i> [Romans] were essentially the inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Aquitania" title="Gallia Aquitania">Aquitaine</a> after that".<sup id="cite_ref-wick123_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wick123-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythological_origins">Mythological origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mythological origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Apart from the more respected <i><a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours#History_of_the_Franks" title="Gregory of Tours">History of the Franks</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>, two more colourful early sources that describe the origin of the Franks are a 7th-century work known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_Fredegar" title="Chronicle of Fredegar">Chronicle of Fredegar</a></i> and the anonymous <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Liber_Historiae_Francorum" title="Liber Historiae Francorum">Liber Historiae Francorum</a></i>, written a century later.
</p><p>The author of the <i>Chronicle of Fredegar</i> claimed that the Franks came originally from <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> and quoted the works of <a href="/wiki/Vergil" class="mw-redirect" title="Vergil">Vergil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Hieronymous</a>, and the Franks are mentioned in those works, by Hieronymous.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The chronicle describes <a href="/wiki/Priam" title="Priam">Priam</a> as a Frankish king whose people migrated to <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a> after the fall of Troy. In Macedonia, the Franks then divided. The European Franks reached Francia under King Francio, just as <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a> went to Rome. Fredegar stated that <a href="/wiki/Theudemer" class="mw-redirect" title="Theudemer">Theudemer</a>, named king of the Franks by Gregory, was descended from <a href="/wiki/Priam" title="Priam">Priam</a>, Friga and Francio.
</p><p>Another work, the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Gesta</i>, described how 12,000 Trojans, led by Priam and <a href="/wiki/Antenor_(mythology)" title="Antenor (mythology)">Antenor</a>, sailed from Troy to the <a href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Don River (Russia)">River Don</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and on to <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>, which is on the River <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, settling near the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a>. There they founded a city called Sicambria. (The <a href="/wiki/Sicambri" title="Sicambri">Sicambri</a> were the most well-known tribe in the Frankish homeland in the time of the early Roman empire, still remembered though defeated and dispersed long before the Frankish name appeared.) The Trojans joined the Roman army in accomplishing the task of driving their enemies into the marshes of Mæotis, for which they received the name of Franks (meaning "savage"). A decade later the Romans killed Priam and drove away <a href="/wiki/Marcomer" title="Marcomer">Marcomer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunno" title="Sunno">Sunno</a>, the sons of Priam and Antenor, and the other Franks.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The major primary sources on the early Franks include the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Panegyrici_Latini" title="Panegyrici Latini">Panegyrici Latini</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudian" title="Claudian">Claudian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zosimus" title="Zosimus">Zosimus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris" title="Sidonius Apollinaris">Sidonius Apollinaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>. The Franks are first mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Augustan History</a></i>, a collection of biographies of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperors</a>. None of these sources present a detailed list of which tribes or parts of tribes became Frankish, or concerning the politics and history, but to quote <a href="#CITEREFEdwards1988">Edwards (1988</a>, p.&#160;35):
</p>
<dl><dd>A Roman marching-song joyfully recorded in a fourth-century source, is associated with the 260s; but the Franks' first appearance in a contemporary source was in 289. [...] The <a href="/wiki/Chamavi" title="Chamavi">Chamavi</a> were mentioned as a Frankish people as early as 289, the <a href="/wiki/Bructeri" title="Bructeri">Bructeri</a> from 307, the <a href="/wiki/Chattuarri" class="mw-redirect" title="Chattuarri">Chattuarri</a> from 306–315, the Salii or Salians from 357, and the <a href="/wiki/Ampsivarii" title="Ampsivarii">Amsivarii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tubantes" title="Tubantes">Tubantes</a> from c. 364–375.</dd></dl>
<p>In 288 the emperor <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> defeated the <a href="/wiki/Salian_Franks" title="Salian Franks">Salian Franks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chamavi" title="Chamavi">Chamavi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frisians" title="Frisians">Frisians</a> and other Germanic people living along the Rhine and moved them to <a href="/wiki/Germania_inferior" class="mw-redirect" title="Germania inferior">Germania inferior</a> to provide manpower and prevent the settlement of other Germanic tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-W5051_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W5051-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BCE7_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCE7-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 292 <a href="/wiki/Constantius_Chlorus" title="Constantius Chlorus">Constantius</a>, the father of Constantine I <sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> defeated the Franks who had settled at the mouth of the Rhine. These were moved to the nearby region of <a href="/wiki/Toxandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxandria">Toxandria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eumenius" title="Eumenius">Eumenius</a> mentions Constantius as having "killed, expelled, captured [and] kidnapped" the Franks who had settled there and others who had crossed the Rhine, using the term <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">nationes Franciae</i> for the first time.
</p><p>It seems likely that the term Frank in this first period had a broader meaning, sometimes including coastal <a href="/wiki/Frisians" title="Frisians">Frisians</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-palaeo_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-palaeo-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Franks were described in Roman texts both as allies (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Laeti" title="Laeti">laeti</a></i>) and enemies (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Dediticii" title="Dediticii">dediticii</a></i>). About the year 260 one group of Franks penetrated as far as <a href="/wiki/Tarragona" title="Tarragona">Tarragona</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, where they plagued the region for about a decade before they were subdued and expelled by the Romans. In 287 or 288, the Roman Caesar <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> forced a Frankish leader <a href="/wiki/Genobaud_(3rd_century)" title="Genobaud (3rd century)">Genobaud</a> and his people to surrender without a fight. Maximian then forced the Salians in Toxandria (the present <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>) to accept imperial authority, but was not able to follow on this success by reconquering Britain.
</p><p>The <i>Life of Aurelian</i>, which was possibly written by Vopiscus, mentions that in 328, Frankish raiders were captured by the 6th Legion stationed at <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>. As a result of this incident, 700 Franks were killed and 300 were sold into slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Frankish incursions over the Rhine became so frequent that the Romans began to settle the Franks on their borders in order to control them.
</p><p>The Franks are mentioned in the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Tabula_Peutingeriana" title="Tabula Peutingeriana">Tabula Peutingeriana</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_road" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman road">Roman roads</a>. It is a 13th-century copy of a 4th or 5th century document that reflects information from the 3rd century. The Romans knew the shape of Europe, but their knowledge is not evident from the map, which was only a practical guide to the roads to be followed from point to point. In the middle Rhine region of the map, the word <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> is close to a misspelling of <a href="/wiki/Bructeri" title="Bructeri">Bructeri</a>. Beyond Mainz is Suevia, the country of the <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a>, and beyond that is Alamannia, the country of the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a>. Four tribes at the mouth of the Rhine are depicted: the <a href="/wiki/Chauci" title="Chauci">Chauci</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ampsivarii" title="Ampsivarii">Amsivarii</a> ('Ems dwellers'), the <a href="/wiki/Cherusci" title="Cherusci">Cherusci</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chamavi" title="Chamavi">Chamavi</a>, followed by <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">qui et Pranci</i> ('who are also Franks'). This implies that the Chamavi were considered Franks. The <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Tabula</i> was probably based on the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Orbis Pictus</i>, a map of twenty years' labour commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> and then kept by the Roman's treasury department for the assessment of taxes. It did not survive as such. Information about the imperial divisions of Gaul probably derives from it.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg/220px-TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg/330px-TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg/440px-TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1290" data-file-height="930" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TabulaPeutingeriana_with_Francia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_Peutingeriana" title="Tabula Peutingeriana">Tabula Peutingeriana</a></i>, showing Francia at the top</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salians">Salians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Salians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salian_Franks" title="Salian Franks">Salian Franks</a></div>
<p>The Salians were first mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a>, who described <a href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian</a>'s defeat of "the first Franks of all, those whom custom has called the Salians," in 358.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Julian allowed the Franks to remain in <a href="/wiki/Toxandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxandria">Texuandria</a> as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">fœderati</i> within the Empire, having moved there from the Rhine-Maas delta.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The 5th century <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Notitia_Dignitatum" title="Notitia Dignitatum">Notitia Dignitatum</a></i> lists a group of soldiers as <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Salii</i>.
</p><p>Some decades later, Franks in the same region, possibly the Salians, controlled the River <a href="/wiki/Scheldt" title="Scheldt">Scheldt</a> and were disrupting transport links to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britain</a> in the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>. Although Roman forces managed to pacify them, they failed to expel the Franks, who continued to be feared as pirates.
</p><p>The Salians are generally seen as the predecessors of the Franks who pushed southwestwards into what is now modern France, who eventually came to be ruled by the Merovingians (see below). This is because when the Merovingian dynasty published the Salian law (<i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Salica" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex Salica">Lex Salica</a></i>) it applied in the Neustrian area from the river Liger (<a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">Loire</a>) to the <i>Silva Carbonaria</i>, the western kingdom founded by them outside the original area of Frankish settlement. In the 5th century Franks under <a href="/wiki/Chlodio" title="Chlodio">Chlodio</a> pushed into Roman lands in and beyond the <i>"<a href="/wiki/Silva_Carbonaria" title="Silva Carbonaria">Silva Carbonaria</a>"</i> or "Charcoal forest", which ran through the area of modern western <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">Wallonia</a>. The forest was the boundary of the original Salian territories to the north and the more Romanized area to the south in the Roman province of <i>Belgica Secunda</i> (roughly equivalent to what Julius Caesar had long ago called "Belgium"). Chlodio conquered <a href="/wiki/Tournai" title="Tournai">Tournai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artois" title="Artois">Artois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambrai" title="Cambrai">Cambrai</a>, and as far as the <a href="/wiki/Somme_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Somme river">Somme river</a>. Chlodio is often seen as an ancestor of the future Merovingian dynasty. Later <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a>, who <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> later reported to be a reputed descendant of Chlodio, was seen as administrative ruler over Roman <i>Belgica Secunda</i> and possibly other areas.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Records of Childeric show him to have been active together with Roman forces in the Loire region, quite far to the south. His descendants came to rule Roman Gaul all the way to there, and this became the Frankish kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Neustria" title="Neustria">Neustria</a>, the basis of what would become medieval <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. Childeric's son <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> also took control of the more independent Frankish kingdoms east of the Silva Carbonaria and Belgica II. This later became the Frankish kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Austrasia</a>, where the early legal code was referred to as "Ripuarian".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ripuarians">Ripuarians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ripuarians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_Franks" title="Ripuarian Franks">Ripuarian Franks</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carte_des_peuples_francs_(IIIe_si%C3%A8cle).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg/220px-Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg/330px-Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg/440px-Carte_des_peuples_francs_%28IIIe_si%C3%A8cle%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="1751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carte_des_peuples_francs_(IIIe_si%C3%A8cle).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate location of the original Frankish tribes in the 3rd century</div></div></div>
<p>The Rhineland Franks who lived near the stretch of the Rhine from roughly <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a> to <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">Duisburg</a>, the region of the city of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, are often considered separately from the Salians, and sometimes in modern texts referred to as Ripuarian Franks. The <a href="/wiki/Ravenna_Cosmography" title="Ravenna Cosmography">Ravenna Cosmography</a> suggests that <i>Francia Renensis</i> included the old <i>civitas</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Ubii" title="Ubii">Ubii</a>, in Germania II (<a href="/wiki/Germania_Inferior" title="Germania Inferior">Germania Inferior</a>), but also the northern part of Germania I (Germania Superior), including <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>. Like the Salians they appear in Roman records both as raiders and as contributors to military units. Unlike the Salii, there is no record of when, if ever, the empire officially accepted their residence within the empire. They eventually succeeded to hold the city of Cologne, and at some point seem to have acquired the name Ripuarians, which may have meant "river people". In any case a Merovingian legal code was called the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Ribuaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex Ribuaria">Lex Ribuaria</a></i>, but it probably applied in all the older Frankish lands, including the original Salian areas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jordanes" title="Jordanes">Jordanes</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Getica_(Jordanes)" class="mw-redirect" title="Getica (Jordanes)">Getica</a></i> mentions the Riparii as auxiliaries of <a href="/wiki/Flavius_Aetius" title="Flavius Aetius">Flavius Aetius</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ch%C3%A2lons" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Châlons">Battle of Châlons</a> in 451: <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">"Hi enim affuerunt auxiliares: Franci, Sarmatae, Armoriciani, Liticiani, Burgundiones, Saxones, Riparii, <a href="/wiki/Olibrones" title="Olibrones">Olibriones</a>&#160;..."</i> <sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> But these <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Riparii</i> ("river dwellers") are today not considered to be Ripuarian Franks, but a known military unit based on the <a href="/wiki/River_Rhone" class="mw-redirect" title="River Rhone">river Rhone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Their territory on both sides of the Rhine became a central part of Merovingian <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Austrasia</a>, which stretched to include Roman <a href="/wiki/Germania_Inferior" title="Germania Inferior">Germania Inferior</a> (later <i>Germania Secunda</i>, which included the original Salian and Ripuarian lands, and roughly equates to medieval Lower Lotharingia) as well as <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a> Prima (late Roman "Belgium", roughly medieval Upper Lotharingia), and lands on the east bank of the Rhine.
</p>
<h3><span id="Merovingian_kingdom_.28481.E2.80.93751.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Merovingian_kingdom_(481–751)">Merovingian kingdom (481–751)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Merovingian kingdom (481–751)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Franks%22">"Franks"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Franks%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Franks%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Franks%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Franks%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Franks%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2007</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Merovingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingians">Merovingians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg/150px-Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg/225px-Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg/300px-Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankish_-_Necklace_-_Walters_47596_-_View_A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 6th–7th century necklace of glass and ceramic beads with a central amethyst bead. Similar necklaces have been found in the graves of Frankish women in the Rhineland.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg/150px-Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg/225px-Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg/300px-Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1329" data-file-height="1799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frankish_-_Digitated_Fibula_-_Walters_542443.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 6th century bow <a href="/wiki/Fibula_(brooch)" title="Fibula (brooch)">fibula</a> found in north-eastern France and the Rhineland. They were worn by Frankish noblewomen in pairs at the shoulder or as belt ornaments.</div></div></div>
<p>Gregory of Tours (Book II) reported that small Frankish kingdoms existed during the fifth century around <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tournai" title="Tournai">Tournai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambrai" title="Cambrai">Cambrai</a> and elsewhere. The kingdom of the Merovingians eventually came to dominate the others, possibly because of its association with Roman power structures in northern Gaul, which the Frankish military forces were apparently integrated into to some extent. <a href="/wiki/Aegidius" title="Aegidius">Aegidius</a>, was originally the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Magister_militum" title="Magister militum">magister militum</a></i> of northern Gaul appointed by <a href="/wiki/Majorian" title="Majorian">Majorian</a>, but after Majorian's death apparently seen as a Roman rebel who relied on Frankish forces. <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> reported that <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a> was exiled for 8 years while Aegidius held the title of "King of the Franks". Eventually Childeric returned and took the same title. Aegidius died in 464 or 465.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Childeric and his son Clovis I were both described as rulers of the Roman Province of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Belgica_Secunda" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgica Secunda">Belgica Secunda</a></i>, by its spiritual leader in the time of Clovis, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Remigius" title="Saint Remigius">Saint Remigius</a>.
</p><p>Clovis later defeated the son of Aegidius, <a href="/wiki/Syagrius" title="Syagrius">Syagrius</a>, in 486 or 487 and then had the Frankish king <a href="/wiki/Chararic_(Frankish_king)" title="Chararic (Frankish king)">Chararic</a> imprisoned and executed. A few years later, he killed <a href="/wiki/Ragnachar" title="Ragnachar">Ragnachar</a>, the Frankish king of Cambrai, and his brothers.  After conquering the <a href="/wiki/Domain_of_Soissons" class="mw-redirect" title="Domain of Soissons">Kingdom of Soissons</a> and expelling the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a> from southern Gaul at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vouill%C3%A9" title="Battle of Vouillé">Battle of Vouillé</a>, he established Frankish hegemony over most of Gaul, excluding <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burgundy" title="Kingdom of Burgundy">Burgundy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, which were eventually absorbed by his successors. By the 490s, he had conquered all the Frankish kingdoms to the west of the <a href="/wiki/River_Maas" class="mw-redirect" title="River Maas">River Maas</a> except for the <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_Franks" title="Ripuarian Franks">Ripuarian Franks</a> and was in a position to make the city of <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> his capital. He became the first king of all Franks in 509, after he had conquered Cologne.
</p><p>Clovis I divided his realm between his four sons, who united to defeat Burgundy in 534. Internecine feuding occurred during the reigns of the brothers <a href="/wiki/Sigebert_I" title="Sigebert I">Sigebert I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chilperic_I" title="Chilperic I">Chilperic I</a>, which was largely fuelled by the rivalry of their queens, <a href="/wiki/Brunhilda_of_Austrasia" title="Brunhilda of Austrasia">Brunhilda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fredegunda" class="mw-redirect" title="Fredegunda">Fredegunda</a>, and which continued during the reigns of their sons and their grandsons. Three distinct subkingdoms emerged: <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Austrasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neustria" title="Neustria">Neustria</a> and Burgundy, each of which developed independently and sought to exert influence over the others. The influence of the <a href="/wiki/Arnulfing" class="mw-redirect" title="Arnulfing">Arnulfing</a> clan of Austrasia ensured that the political centre of gravity in the kingdom gradually shifted eastwards to the Rhineland.
</p><p>The Frankish realm was reunited in 613 by <a href="/wiki/Chlothar_II" title="Chlothar II">Chlothar II</a>, the son of Chilperic, who granted his nobles the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Paris" title="Edict of Paris">Edict of Paris</a> in an effort to reduce corruption and reassert his authority. Following the military successes of his son and successor <a href="/wiki/Dagobert_I" title="Dagobert I">Dagobert I</a>, royal authority rapidly declined under a series of kings, traditionally known as <i>les <a href="/wiki/Rois_fain%C3%A9ants" class="mw-redirect" title="Rois fainéants">rois fainéants</a></i>. After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tertry" title="Battle of Tertry">Battle of Tertry</a> in 687, each <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_the_palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayor of the palace">mayor of the palace</a>, who had formerly been the king's chief household official, effectively held power until in 751, with the approval of the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> and the nobility, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> deposed the last Merovingian king <a href="/wiki/Childeric_III" title="Childeric III">Childeric III</a> and had himself crowned. This inaugurated a new dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Carolingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingians">Carolingians</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Carolingian_empire_.28751.E2.80.93843.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Carolingian_empire_(751–843)">Carolingian empire (751–843)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Carolingian empire (751–843)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a></div>
<p>The unification achieved by the Merovingians ensured the continuation of what has become known as the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a>. The Carolingian Empire was beset by internecine warfare, but the combination of Frankish rule and Roman Christianity ensured that it was fundamentally united. Frankish government and culture depended very much upon each ruler and his aims and so each region of the empire developed differently. Although a ruler's aims depended upon the political alliances of his family, the leading families of Francia shared the same basic beliefs and ideas of government, which had both Roman and Germanic roots.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The Frankish state consolidated its hold over the majority of western Europe by the end of the 8th century, developing into the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a>. With the <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne#Coronation" title="Charlemagne">coronation of their ruler Charlemagne</a> as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> in 800 AD, he and his successors were recognised as legitimate successors to the emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. As such, the Carolingian Empire gradually came to be seen in the West as a continuation of the ancient Roman Empire. This empire would give rise to several successor states, including France, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a>, though the <i>Frankish</i> identity remained most closely identified with France.
</p><p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, his only adult surviving son became Emperor and King <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a>. Following Louis the Pious's death, however, according to Frankish culture and law that demanded equality among all living male adult heirs, the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a> was now split between Louis' three sons.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Participation_in_the_Roman_army">Participation in the Roman army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Participation in the Roman army">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Germanic peoples, including those tribes in the Rhine delta that later became the Franks, are known to have served in the Roman army since the days of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>. After the Roman administration collapsed in Gaul in the 260s, the armies under the Germanic Batavian <a href="/wiki/Postumus" title="Postumus">Postumus</a> revolted and proclaimed him emperor and then restored order. From then on, Germanic soldiers in the Roman army, most notably Franks, were promoted from the ranks. A few decades later, the Menapian <a href="/wiki/Carausius" title="Carausius">Carausius</a> created a Batavian–British rump state on Roman soil that was supported by Frankish soldiers and raiders. Frankish soldiers such as <a href="/wiki/Magnentius" title="Magnentius">Magnentius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Silvanus" title="Claudius Silvanus">Silvanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arbitio" title="Arbitio">Arbitio</a> held command positions in the Roman army during the mid 4th century. From the narrative of <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a> it is evident that both Frankish and Alamannic tribal armies were organised along Roman lines.
</p><p>After the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Chlodio" title="Chlodio">Chlodio</a>, the Roman armies at the Rhine border became a Frankish "franchise" and Franks were known to levy Roman-like troops that were supported by a Roman-like armour and weapons industry. This lasted at least until the days of the scholar <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a> (c. 500 – c. 565), more than a century after the demise of the Western Roman Empire, who wrote describing the former Rhine army as still in operation with legions of the style of their forefathers during Roman times. The Franks under the Merovingians melded Germanic custom with Romanised organisation and several important tactical innovations. Before their conquest of Gaul, the Franks fought primarily as a tribe, unless they were part of a Roman military unit fighting in conjunction with other imperial units.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_practices_of_the_early_Franks">Military practices of the early Franks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Military practices of the early Franks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary sources for Frankish military custom and armament are <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agathias" title="Agathias">Agathias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>, the latter two <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman</a> historians writing about Frankish intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_War_(535%E2%80%93552)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic War (535–552)">Gothic War</a>.
</p><p>Writing of 539, Procopius says:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>At this time the Franks, hearing that both the Goths and Romans had suffered severely by the war&#160;... forgetting for the moment their oaths and treaties&#160;... (for this nation in matters of trust is the most treacherous in the world), they straightway gathered to the number of one hundred thousand under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Theudebert_I" title="Theudebert I">Theudebert I</a> and marched into Italy: they had a small body of cavalry about their leader, and these were the only ones armed with spears, while all the rest were foot soldiers having neither bows nor spears, but each man carried a sword and shield and one axe. Now the iron head of this weapon was thick and exceedingly sharp on both sides, while the wooden handle was very short. And they are accustomed always to throw these axes at a signal in the first charge and thus to shatter the shields of the enemy and kill the men.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>His contemporary, Agathias, who based his own writings upon the tropes laid down by Procopius, says:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The military equipment of this people [the Franks] is very simple&#160;... They do not know the use of the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_mail" class="mw-redirect" title="Coat of mail">coat of mail</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greave" title="Greave">greaves</a> and the majority leave the head uncovered, only a few wear the helmet. They have their chests bare and backs naked to the loins, they cover their thighs with either leather or linen. They do not serve on horseback except in very rare cases. Fighting on foot is both habitual and a national custom and they are proficient in this. At the hip they wear a sword and on the left side their shield is attached. They have neither bows nor slings, no <a href="/wiki/Ranged_weapon" title="Ranged weapon">missile weapons</a> except the double edged axe and the <a href="/wiki/Angon" title="Angon">angon</a> which they use most often. The angons are spears which are neither very short nor very long. They can be used, if necessary, for throwing like a <a href="/wiki/Javelin" title="Javelin">javelin</a>, and also in <a href="/wiki/Hand_to_hand_combat" class="mw-redirect" title="Hand to hand combat">hand to hand combat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>While the above quotations have been used as a statement of the military practices of the Frankish nation in the 6th century and have even been extrapolated to the entire period preceding <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a>'s reforms (early mid-8th century), post-Second World War historiography has emphasised the inherited Roman characteristics of the Frankish military from the date of the beginning of the conquest of Gaul. The Byzantine authors present several contradictions and difficulties. Procopius denies the Franks the use of the spear while Agathias makes it one of their primary weapons. They agree that the Franks were primarily infantrymen, threw axes and carried a sword and shield. Both writers also contradict the authority of Gallic authors of the same general time period (<a href="/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris" title="Sidonius Apollinaris">Sidonius Apollinaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a>) and the archaeological evidence. The <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Lex_Ribuaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex Ribuaria">Lex Ribuaria</a></i>, the early 7th century legal code of the Rhineland or Ripuarian Franks, specifies the values of various goods when paying a <a href="/wiki/Wergild" class="mw-redirect" title="Wergild">wergild</a> in kind; whereas a spear and shield were worth only two <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Solidi" class="mw-redirect" title="Solidi">solidi</a></i>, a sword and scabbard were valued at seven, a helmet at six, and a "metal tunic" at twelve.<sup id="cite_ref-James_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scramasax" class="mw-redirect" title="Scramasax">Scramasaxes</a> and arrowheads are numerous in Frankish graves even though the Byzantine historians do not assign them to the Franks.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours,_Histoire_des_Francs,_livres_1_%C3%A0_6,_page_de_frontispice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg/220px-Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg/330px-Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg/440px-Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours%2C_Histoire_des_Francs%2C_livres_1_%C3%A0_6%2C_page_de_frontispice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="472" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A9goire_de_Tours,_Histoire_des_Francs,_livres_1_%C3%A0_6,_page_de_frontispice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The frontispiece of Gregory's <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Historia Francorum</i></div></div></div>
<p>The evidence of Gregory and of the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Lex_Salica" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex Salica">Lex Salica</a></i> implies that the early Franks were a cavalry people. In fact, some modern historians have hypothesised that the Franks possessed so numerous a body of horses that they could use them to plough fields and thus were agriculturally technologically advanced over their neighbours. The <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Lex_Ribuaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex Ribuaria">Lex Ribuaria</a></i> specifies that a mare's value was the same as that of an ox or of a shield and spear, two <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">solidi</i> and a stallion seven or the same as a sword and scabbard,<sup id="cite_ref-James_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> which suggests that horses were relatively common. Perhaps the Byzantine writers considered the Frankish horse to be insignificant relative to the Greek cavalry, which is probably accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merovingian_military">Merovingian military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Merovingian military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_and_development">Composition and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Composition and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Frankish military establishment incorporated many of the pre-existing Roman institutions in Gaul, especially during and after the conquests of Clovis I in the late 5th and early 6th centuries. Frankish military strategy revolved around the holding and taking of fortified centres (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">castra</i>) and in general these centres were held by garrisons of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">milities</i> or <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Laeti" title="Laeti">laeti</a></i>, who were former Roman mercenaries of Germanic origin. Throughout Gaul, the descendants of Roman soldiers continued to wear their uniforms and perform their ceremonial duties.
</p><p>Immediately beneath the Frankish king in the military hierarchy were the <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">leudes</i>, his sworn followers, who were generally 'old soldiers' in service away from court.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The king had an elite bodyguard called the <i><a href="/wiki/Truste" class="mw-redirect" title="Truste">truste</a></i>. Members of the <i>truste</i> often served in <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">centannae</i>, garrison settlements that were established for military and police purposes. The day-to-day bodyguard of the king was made up of <i><a href="/wiki/Antrustiones" class="mw-redirect" title="Antrustiones">antrustiones</a></i> (senior soldiers who were <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocrats</a> in military service) and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">pueri</i> (junior soldiers and not aristocrats).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> All high-ranking men had <i>pueri</i>.
</p><p>The Frankish military was not composed solely of Franks and Gallo-Romans, but also contained <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taifals" title="Taifals">Taifals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>. After the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burgundy" title="Kingdom of Burgundy">Burgundy</a> (534), the well-organised military institutions of that kingdom were integrated into the Frankish realm. Chief among these was the standing army under the command of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Burgundy" title="Duke of Burgundy">Patrician of Burgundy</a>.
</p><p>In the late 6th century, during the wars instigated by <a href="/wiki/Fredegund" title="Fredegund">Fredegund</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brunhilda_of_Austrasia" title="Brunhilda of Austrasia">Brunhilda</a>, the Merovingian monarchs introduced a new element into their militaries: the local <a href="/wiki/Feudal_levies" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal levies">levy</a>. A levy consisted of all the able-bodied men of a district who were required to report for military service when called upon, similar to <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a>. The local levy applied only to a city and its environs. Initially only in certain cities in western Gaul, in Neustria and Aquitaine, did the kings possess the right or power to call up the levy. The commanders of the local levies were always different from the commanders of the urban garrisons. Often the former were commanded by the <a href="/wiki/Comes" title="Comes">counts</a> of the districts. A much rarer occurrence was the general levy, which applied to the entire kingdom and included peasants (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text">pauperes</i> and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">inferiores</i>). General levies could also be made within the still-pagan trans-Rhenish <a href="/wiki/Stem_duchies" class="mw-redirect" title="Stem duchies">stem duchies</a> on the orders of a monarch. The <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, Alemanni and <a href="/wiki/Thuringii" title="Thuringii">Thuringii</a> all had the institution of the levy and the Frankish monarchs could depend upon their levies until the mid-7th century, when the stem dukes began to sever their ties to the monarchy. <a href="/wiki/Radulf,_King_of_Thuringia" title="Radulf, King of Thuringia">Radulf of Thuringia</a> called up the levy for a war against <a href="/wiki/Sigebert_III" title="Sigebert III">Sigebert III</a> in 640.
</p><p>Soon the local levy spread to Austrasia and the less Romanised regions of Gaul. On an intermediate level, the kings began calling up territorial levies from the regions of Austrasia (which did not have major cities of Roman origin). All the forms of the levy gradually disappeared, however, in the course of the 7th century after the reign of <a href="/wiki/Dagobert_I" title="Dagobert I">Dagobert I</a>. Under the so-called <i lang="fr" title="French language text"><a href="/wiki/Rois_fain%C3%A9ants" class="mw-redirect" title="Rois fainéants">rois fainéants</a></i>, the levies disappeared by mid-century in Austrasia and later in Burgundy and Neustria. Only in Aquitaine, which was fast becoming independent of the central Frankish monarchy, did complex military institutions persist into the 8th century. In the final half of the 7th century and first half of the 8th in Merovingian Gaul, the chief military actors became the lay and ecclesiastical <a href="/wiki/Magnate" title="Magnate">magnates</a> with their bands of armed followers called retainers. The other aspects of the Merovingian military, mostly Roman in origin or innovations of powerful kings, disappeared from the scene by the 8th century.
</p>
<h4><span id="Strategy.2C_tactics_and_equipment"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategy,_tactics_and_equipment">Strategy, tactics and equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Strategy, tactics and equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Merovingian armies used <a href="/wiki/Hauberk" title="Hauberk">coats of mail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helmet" title="Helmet">helmets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shield" title="Shield">shields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lance" title="Lance">lances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sword" title="Sword">swords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bow_and_arrow" title="Bow and arrow">bows and arrows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">war horses</a>. The armament of private armies resembled those of the Gallo-Roman <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">potentiatores</i> of the late Empire. A strong element of Alanic cavalry settled in <a href="/wiki/Armorica" title="Armorica">Armorica</a> influenced the fighting style of the <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a> down into the 12th century. Local urban levies could be reasonably well-armed and even mounted, but the more general levies were composed of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">pauperes</i> and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">inferiores</i>, who were mostly farmers by trade and carried ineffective weapons, such as farming implements. The peoples east of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>&#160;– Franks, Saxons and even <a href="/wiki/Wends" title="Wends">Wends</a>&#160;– who were sometimes called upon to serve, wore rudimentary armour and carried weapons such as <a href="/wiki/Spear" title="Spear">spears</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axe" title="Axe">axes</a>. Few of these men were mounted.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Merovingian society had a militarised nature. The Franks called annual meetings every <a href="/w/index.php?title=Marchfield_(Frankish)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Marchfield (Frankish) (page does not exist)">Marchfeld</a> (1 March), when the king and his nobles assembled in large open fields and determined their targets for the next campaigning season. The meetings were a show of strength on behalf of the monarch and a way for him to retain loyalty among his troops.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In their civil wars, the Merovingian kings concentrated on the holding of fortified places and the use of <a href="/wiki/Siege_engine" title="Siege engine">siege engines</a>. In wars waged against external foes, the objective was typically the acquisition of booty or the enforcement of tribute. Only in the lands beyond the Rhine did the Merovingians seek to extend political control over their neighbours.
</p><p>Tactically, the Merovingians borrowed heavily from the Romans, especially regarding siege warfare. Their battle tactics were highly flexible and were designed to meet the specific circumstances of a battle. The tactic of subterfuge was employed endlessly. Cavalry formed a large segment of an army<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Frankish Cavalry did not become significant until after the battle of Tours and Charles Martel&#39;s reforms due to experiencing contact with the Muslim Ummayad cavalry and witnessing its effectiveness, Tours was after the Merovingian dynasty, stating cavalry formed a large segment of the army is misleading, it was heavily infantry based and in the main horses were used as transport only (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, but troops readily dismounted to fight on foot. The Merovingians were capable of raising naval forces: the naval campaign waged against the <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danes</a> by <a href="/wiki/Theuderic_I" title="Theuderic I">Theuderic I</a> in 515 involved ocean-worthy ships and rivercraft were used on the <a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">Loire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish language</a></div>
<p>In a modern <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistic</a> context, the language of the early Franks is variously called "Old Frankish" or "Old Franconian" and refers to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> dialects of the Franks prior to the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Germanic_consonant_shift" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Germanic consonant shift">Second Germanic consonant shift</a>, which took place between 600 and 700. After this consonant shift the Frankish dialect diverges, with the dialects that would become modern <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> not undergoing the consonantal shift, while all others did so <a href="/wiki/Rhenish_fan" title="Rhenish fan">to varying degrees</a> and thereby became part of the larger <a href="/wiki/High_German" class="mw-redirect" title="High German">German dialectal domain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Frankish language has not been directly attested, apart from a very small number of <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">runic inscriptions</a> found within contemporary Frankish territory such as the <a href="/wiki/Bergakker_inscription" title="Bergakker inscription">Bergakker inscription</a>. The distinction between <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">Old Dutch</a> and Old Frankish is largely negligible, with Old Dutch (also called <a href="/wiki/Old_Low_Franconian" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Low Franconian">Old Low Franconian</a>) being the term used to differentiate between the affected and non-affected variants following the aforementioned Second Germanic consonant shift.<sup id="cite_ref-mees_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mees-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant amount of Old Frankish vocabulary has been reconstructed by examining early Germanic loanwords found in <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> as well as through <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">comparative reconstruction</a> through Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The influence of Old Frankish on contemporary <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallo-Roman">Gallo-Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, have long been questions of scholarly debate.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Frankish influence is thought to include the designations of  the four cardinal directions: <i>nord</i> "north", <i>sud</i> "south", <i>est</i> "east" and <i>ouest</i> "west" and at least an additional 1000 stem words.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica.com_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Franks would eventually conquer all of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, speakers of Old Franconian apparently expanded in sufficient numbers only into northern Gaul to have a linguistic effect. For several centuries, northern Gaul was a bilingual territory (<a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> and Franconian). The language used in writing, in government and by the Church was Latin. <a href="/wiki/Urban_T._Holmes,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Urban T. Holmes, Jr.">Urban T. Holmes</a> has proposed that a Germanic language continued to be spoken as a second tongue by public officials in western <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Austrasia</a> and Northern <a href="/wiki/Neustria" title="Neustria">Neustria</a> as late as the 850s, and that it completely disappeared as a spoken language from regions where French is spoken today only during the 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG/120px-Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG" decoding="async" width="120" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG/180px-Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG/240px-Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1308" data-file-height="1589" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tr%C3%A9sor_de_Gourdon_04.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Chalice" title="Chalice">chalice</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Gourdon" title="Treasure of Gourdon">Treasure of Gourdon</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg/220px-Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg/330px-Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg/440px-Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5912" data-file-height="3916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Dom_BW_2016-07-09_16-20-40.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pinnacle of Carolingian architecture: the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel_in_Aachen" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatine Chapel in Aachen">palatine chapel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a>, Germany.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_art_and_architecture" title="Merovingian art and architecture">Merovingian art and architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian art</a></div>
<p>Early Frankish art and architecture belongs to a phase known as <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period_art" title="Migration Period art">Migration Period art</a>, which has left very few remains. The later period is called <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian art</a>, or, especially in <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pre-Romanesque" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Romanesque">pre-Romanesque</a>. Very little Merovingian architecture has been preserved. The earliest churches seem to have been timber-built, with larger examples being of a <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> type. The most completely surviving example, a <a href="/wiki/Baptistery" title="Baptistery">baptistery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">Poitiers</a>, is a building with three <a href="/wiki/Apse" title="Apse">apses</a> of a Gallo-Roman style.
