A number of small <a href="/wiki/Baptistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptistry">baptistries</a> can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">Southern France</a>: as these fell out of fashion, they were not updated and have subsequently survived as they were.
</p><p>Jewelry (such as brooches), weapons (including swords with decorative hilts) and clothing (such as capes and sandals) have been found in a number of grave sites. The grave of Queen <a href="/wiki/Aregund" title="Aregund">Aregund</a>, discovered in 1959, and the <a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Gourdon" title="Treasure of Gourdon">Treasure of Gourdon</a>, which was deposited soon after 524, are notable examples. The few Merovingian <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscripts" class="mw-redirect" title="Illuminated manuscripts">illuminated manuscripts</a> that have survived, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gelasian_Sacramentary" title="Gelasian Sacramentary">Gelasian Sacramentary</a>, contain a great deal of <a href="/wiki/Zoomorphism" title="Zoomorphism">zoomorphic representations</a>. Such Frankish objects show a greater use of the style and motifs of <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and a lesser degree of skill and sophistication in design and manufacture than comparable works from the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>. So little has survived, however, that the best quality of work from this period may not be represented.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The objects produced by the main centres of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a>, which represent a transformation from that of the earlier period, have survived in far greater quantity. The arts were lavishly funded and encouraged by <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, using imported artists where necessary, and Carolingian developments were decisive for the future course of <a href="/wiki/Western_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Western art">Western art</a>. Carolingian <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> and ivory plaques, which have survived in reasonable numbers, approached those of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in quality. The main surviving monument of <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_architecture" title="Carolingian architecture">Carolingian architecture</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel_in_Aachen" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatine Chapel in Aachen">Palatine Chapel in Aachen</a>, which is an impressive and confident adaptation of <a href="/wiki/San_Vitale,_Ravenna" class="mw-redirect" title="San Vitale, Ravenna">San Vitale, Ravenna</a> – from where some of the pillars were brought. Many other important buildings existed, such as the monasteries of Centula or <a href="/wiki/St_Gall" class="mw-redirect" title="St Gall">St Gall</a>, or the old <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a>, since rebuilt. These large structures and complexes made frequent use of towers.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A sizeable portion of the Frankish aristocracy quickly followed Clovis in converting to Christianity (the Frankish church of the Merovingians). The conversion of all under Frankish rule required a considerable amount of time and effort.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paganism">Paganism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Paganism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Mythology">Frankish Mythology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Childeric%27s_bees.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Childeric%27s_bees.jpg/220px-Childeric%27s_bees.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Childeric%27s_bees.jpg/330px-Childeric%27s_bees.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Childeric%27s_bees.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="349" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Childeric%27s_bees.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing of golden bees or flies that was discovered in the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a></div></div></div>
<p>Echoes of <a href="/wiki/Frankish_mythology" title="Frankish mythology">Frankish paganism</a> can be found in the primary sources, but their meaning is not always clear. Interpretations by modern scholars differ greatly, but it is likely that Frankish paganism shared most of the characteristics of other varieties of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>. The mythology of the Franks was probably a form of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_polytheism" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic polytheism">Germanic polytheism</a>. It was highly ritualistic. Many daily activities centred around the multiple deities, chiefest of which may have been the <a href="/wiki/Quinotaur" title="Quinotaur">Quinotaur</a>, a water-god from whom the Merovingians were reputed to have derived their ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Most of their gods were linked with local cult centres and their sacred character and power were associated with specific regions, outside of which they were neither worshipped nor feared. Most of the gods were "worldly", possessing form and having connections with specific objects, in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Frankish paganism has been observed in the burial site of <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a>, where the king's body was found covered in a cloth decorated with numerous bees. There is a likely connection with the bees to the traditional Frankish weapon, the <a href="/wiki/Angon" title="Angon">angon</a> (meaning "sting"), from its distinctive spearhead. It is possible that the <a href="/wiki/Fleur-de-lis" title="Fleur-de-lis">fleur-de-lis</a> is derived from the angon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg/220px-Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg/330px-Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="379" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bateme_de_Clovis_par_St_Remy-edit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue in the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint-Remi" title="Basilica of Saint-Remi">Basilica of Saint-Remi</a> depicting the baptism of Clovis I by Saint Remi in around 496</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Merovingian_Gaul" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianity in Merovingian Gaul">Christianity in Merovingian Gaul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Church">Frankish Church</a></div>
<p>Some Franks, like the 4th century usurper <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Silvanus" title="Claudius Silvanus">Silvanus</a>, converted early to Christianity. In 496, Clovis I, who had married a Burgundian <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Catholic</a> named <a href="/wiki/Clotilde" title="Clotilde">Clotilda</a> in 493, was baptised by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Remi" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Remi">Saint Remi</a> after a decisive victory over the Alemanni at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tolbiac" title="Battle of Tolbiac">Battle of Tolbiac</a>. According to Gregory of Tours, over three thousand of his soldiers were baptised with him.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Clovis' conversion had a profound effect on the course of European history, for at the time the Franks were the only major <a href="/wiki/Germanic_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Christianity">Christianised Germanic tribe</a> without a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian</a> aristocracy and this led to a naturally amicable relationship between the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and the increasingly powerful Franks.
</p><p>Although many of the Frankish aristocracy quickly followed Clovis in converting to Christianity, the conversion of all his subjects was only achieved after considerable effort and, in some regions, a period of over two centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Chronicle of St. Denis</i> relates that, following Clovis' conversion, a number of pagans who were unhappy with this turn of events rallied around <a href="/wiki/Ragnachar" title="Ragnachar">Ragnachar</a>, who had played an important role in Clovis' initial rise to power. Although the text remains unclear as to the precise pretext, Clovis had Ragnachar executed.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Remaining pockets of resistance were overcome region by region, primarily due to the work of an expanding network of monasteries.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg/200px-Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg/300px-Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg/400px-Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1554" data-file-height="1165" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sacr_Gelasianum_131v_132.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gelasian_Sacramentary" title="Gelasian Sacramentary">Gelasian Sacramentary</a>, c. 750</div></div></div>
<p>The Merovingian Church was shaped by both internal and external forces. It had to come to terms with an established Gallo-Roman hierarchy that resisted changes to its culture, Christianise pagan sensibilities and suppress their expression, provide a new theological basis for Merovingian forms of kingship deeply rooted in pagan Germanic tradition and accommodate Irish and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_mission" title="Anglo-Saxon mission">Anglo-Saxon missionary activities</a> and papal requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The Carolingian reformation of monasticism and church-state relations was the culmination of the Frankish Church.
</p><p>The increasingly wealthy Merovingian elite endowed many monasteries, including that of the Irish missionary <a href="/wiki/Columbanus" title="Columbanus">Columbanus</a>. The 5th, 6th and 7th centuries saw two major waves of <a href="/wiki/Hermit" title="Hermit">hermitism</a> in the Frankish world, which led to legislation requiring that all monks and hermits follow the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_St_Benedict" class="mw-redirect" title="Rule of St Benedict">Rule of St Benedict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Church sometimes had an uneasy relationship with the Merovingian kings, whose claim to rule depended on a mystique of royal descent and who tended to revert to the polygamy of their pagan ancestors. Rome encouraged the Franks to slowly replace the <a href="/wiki/Gallican_Rite" title="Gallican Rite">Gallican Rite</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_rite" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman rite">Roman rite</a>. When the mayors took over, the Church was supportive and an Emperor crowned by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> was much more to their liking.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As with other Germanic peoples, the laws of the Franks were memorised by "rachimburgs", who were analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Lawspeaker" title="Lawspeaker">lawspeakers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> By the 6th century, when these laws first appeared in written form, two basic legal subdivisions existed: Salian Franks were subject to <a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">Salic law</a> and Ripuarian Franks to <a href="/wiki/Lex_Ripuaria" title="Lex Ripuaria">Ripuarian law</a>. Gallo-Romans south of the <a href="/wiki/Loire_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Loire River">River Loire</a> and clergy remained subject to traditional <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Germanic law was overwhelmingly concerned with the protection of individuals and less concerned with protecting the interests of the state. According to Michel Rouche, "Frankish judges devoted as much care to a case involving the theft of a dog as Roman judges did to cases involving the fiscal responsibility of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">curiales</i>, or municipal councilors".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_814.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Europe_814.png/230px-Europe_814.png" decoding="async" width="230" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Europe_814.png/345px-Europe_814.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Europe_814.png/460px-Europe_814.png 2x" data-file-width="1370" data-file-height="1047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_814.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a> (green) in 814</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Frank</i> has been used by many of the Eastern Orthodox and Muslim neighbours of medieval Latin <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> (and beyond, such as in Asia) as a general synonym for a European from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, areas that followed the Latin rites of Christianity under the authority of the Pope in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Another term with similar use was <i><a href="/wiki/Latins_(Middle_Ages)" title="Latins (Middle Ages)">Latins</a></i>.
</p><p>Modern historians often refer to Christians following the Latin rites in the eastern Mediterranean as <i>Franks</i> or <i>Latins</i>, regardless of their country of origin, whereas they use the words <i><a href="/wiki/Rhomaios" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhomaios">Rhomaios</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûmi</a></i> ("Roman") for Orthodox Christians. On a number of Greek islands, Catholics are still referred to as <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Φράγκοι</span> (<i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek language text">Frangoi</i>) or "Franks", for instance on <a href="/wiki/Syros" title="Syros">Syros</a>, where they are called <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Φραγκοσυριανός</span> (<i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek language text">Frangosyrianos</i>).  The period of <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Crusader</a> rule in Greek lands is known to this day as the <i><a href="/wiki/Frangokratia" class="mw-redirect" title="Frangokratia">Frangokratia</a></i> ("rule of the Franks"). Latin Christians living in the Middle East (particularly in the Levant) are known as <a href="/wiki/Franco-Levantines" class="mw-redirect" title="Franco-Levantines">Franco-Levantines</a>.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> in the 13-14th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> used the term "Franks" to designate Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The term <i><a href="/wiki/Frangistan" title="Frangistan">Frangistan</a></i> ("Land of the Franks") was used by Muslims to refer to Christian Europe and was commonly used over several centuries in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.
</p><p>The Chinese called the Portuguese <i lang="pt" title="Portuguese language text">Folangji</i> 佛郎機 ("Franks") in the 1520s at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tunmen" title="Battle of Tunmen">Battle of Tunmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Xicaowan" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Xicaowan">Battle of Xicaowan</a>. Some other varieties of <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a> pronounced the characters as Fah-lan-ki.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>During the reign of Chingtih (<i>Zhengde</i>) (1506), foreigners from the west called Fah-lan-ki (or Franks), who said they had <a href="/wiki/Tribute#Chinese_practice_of_tributes_as_trade_regulation_and_authority" title="Tribute">tribute</a>, abruptly entered the <a href="/wiki/Bocca_Tigris" class="mw-redirect" title="Bocca Tigris">Bogue</a>, and by their tremendously loud guns shook the place far and near. This was reported at court, and an order returned to drive them away immediately, and stop the trade.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Samuel Wells Williams</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Lingua_Franca" title="Mediterranean Lingua Franca">Mediterranean Lingua Franca</a> (or "Frankish language") was a <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> first spoken by 11th century European Christians and Muslims in <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean ports</a> that remained in use until the 19th century.
</p><p>Examples of derived words include:
</p>
<ul><li><i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek language text">Frangos</i> (<span lang="el" title="Greek language text">Φράγκος</span>) in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a></li>
<li><i lang="sq" title="Albanian language text">Frëng</i> in <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a></li>
<li><i lang="tr" title="Turkish language text">Frenk</i> in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a></li>
<li><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">al-Faranj</i>, <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">Afranj</i> and <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">Firinjīyah</i> in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i lang="fa-Latn" title="Persian language text">Farang</i>, <i lang="fa-Latn" title="Persian language text">Farangī</i> in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <i>Faranji</i> in <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AbdullaevAkbarzaheh2010_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AbdullaevAkbarzaheh2010-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Ferengi or Faranji</i> in some Turkic languages</li>
<li><i lang="hi-Latn" title="Hindi language text">Feringhi</i> or <i lang="hi-Latn" title="Hindi language text">Firang</i> in <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Urdu language">Urdu</a> (derived from Persian)</li>
<li><i>Phirangee</i> in some other Indian languages</li>
<li><i lang="ta-Latn" title="Tamil language text">Parangiar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a></li>
<li><i lang="ml-Latn" title="Malayalam language text">Parangi</i> in <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayalam language">Malayalam</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a>, the word refers specifically to <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese people</a></li>
<li><i lang="km-Latn" title="Khmer language text"><a href="/wiki/Barang_(Khmer_word)" title="Barang (Khmer word)">Barang</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Khmer_language" title="Khmer language">Khmer</a></li>
<li><i lang="ms" title="Malay language text">Feringgi</i> in <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a></li>
<li><i lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">Folangji</i><sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson2000_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson2000-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> or <i>Fah-lan-ki</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">佛郎機</span>) and <i>Fulang</i><sup id="cite_ref-Park2012_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Park2012-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a></li>
<li><i lang="th-Latn" title="Thai language text"><a href="/wiki/Farang" title="Farang">Farang</a></i> (<span lang="th" title="Thai language text">ฝรั่ง</span>) in <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i lang="id" title="Indonesian language text">Pirang</i> ("blonde"), <i lang="id" title="Indonesian language text">Perangai</i> ("temperament/al") in <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Bahasa Indonesia</a></li></ul>
<p>In the Thai usage, the word can refer to any European person. When the presence of <a href="/wiki/United_States_military" class="mw-redirect" title="United States military">US soldiers</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> placed Thai people in contact with <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, they (and people of African ancestry in general) came to be called <i lang="id" title="Indonesian language text">Farang dam</i> ("Black Farang", <span lang="th" title="Thai language text">ฝรั่งดำ</span>). Such words sometimes also connote things, plants or creatures introduced by Europeans/Franks. For example, in Khmer, <i lang="km" title="Khmer language text">môn barang</i>, literally "French Chicken", refers to a <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a> and in Thai, <i lang="th-Latn" title="Thai language text"><a href="/wiki/Farang" title="Farang">Farang</a></i> is the name both for Europeans and for the <a href="/wiki/Guava" title="Guava">guava</a> fruit, introduced by Portuguese traders over 400 years ago. In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> word <span lang="yi" dir="rtl" title="Yiddish language text">פרענק</span> (<i lang="yi" title="Yiddish language text">Frenk</i>) has, by a curious etymological development, come to refer to <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mizrahi">Mizrahi</a> Jews and carries a strong pejorative connotation.
</p><p>Some linguists (among them Drs. Jan Tent and Paul Geraghty) have suggested that the <a href="/wiki/Samoan_language" title="Samoan language">Samoan</a> and generic <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian</a> term for Europeans, <i><a href="/wiki/Palagi" title="Palagi">Palagi</a></i> (pronounced Puh-LANG-ee) or <i>Papalagi</i>, might also be cognate, possibly a loan term gathered by early contact between Pacific islanders and Malays.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Franks&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mjollnir_icon.png/21px-Mjollnir_icon.png" decoding="async" width="21" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mjollnir_icon.png/32px-Mjollnir_icon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mjollnir_icon.png/42px-Mjollnir_icon.png 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="263" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Ancient_Germanic_culture" title="Portal:Ancient Germanic culture">Ancient Germanic culture portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Christianity">Germanic Christianity</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings" title="List of Frankish kings">List of Frankish kings</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_queens" title="List of Frankish queens">List of Frankish queens</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the 6th Gallicana is only known from this work, its existence is sometimes questioned along with the genuineness of the work; the question remains unanswered, however: <cite class="citation web">Lendering, Jona. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.livius.org/le-lh/legio/vi_gallicana.html">"Legio VI Gallicana"</a>. Livius.org.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Legio+VI+Gallicana&amp;rft.pub=Livius.org&amp;rft.aulast=Lendering&amp;rft.aufirst=Jona&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.livius.org%2Fle-lh%2Flegio%2Fvi_gallicana.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Res Gestae</i>, XVII.8.</span>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Latin, <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">petit primos omnium Francos, eos videlicet quos consuetudo Salios appellavit</i> is slightly ambiguous, resulting in an interpretation "first of all he proceeded against the Franks&#160;..." with "first" presented improperly as an adjective instead of an adverb. As it stands, the Salians are the first Franks of all; if an adverb is intended, the Franks are they who are the Salians.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Previt%C3%A9-Orton" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles William Previté-Orton">Previté-Orton</a>. <i>The Shorter Cambridge Medieval History, vol. I</i>. pp.&#160;51–52.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Shorter+Cambridge+Medieval+History%2C+vol.+I&amp;rft.pages=51-52&amp;rft.au=Previt%C3%A9-Orton&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFranks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPfister1911">Pfister 1911</a>, p.&#160;296.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gregory of Tours was apparently skeptical of Childeric's connection to Chlodio, and only says that some say there was such a connection. Concerning <i>Belgica Secunda</i>, which Chlodio had conquered first for the Franks, Bishop Remigius, the leader of the church in the same province, stated in a letter to Childeric's son Clovis that "Great news has reached us that you have taken up the administration of <i>Belgica Secunda</i>. It is no surprise that you have begun to be as your parents ever were." (Epistolae Austriacae, translated by AC Murray, and quoted in Murray's "From Roman to Merovingian Gaul" p. 260). This is normally interpreted to mean that Childeric also had this administration. (See for example Wood "The Merovingian Kingdoms" p. 41.) Both the passage of Gregory and the letter of Remigius note the nobility of Clovis's mother when discussing his connection to this area.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Paragraph 191.</span>

</li>
